    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

<span id="freeTextContainer5363830880433433216"><b>Graham Greene's classic exploration of love, innocence, and morality in Vietnam</b><br/><br/>"I never knew a man who had better motives for all the trouble he caused," Graham Greene's narrator Fowler remarks of Alden Pyle, the eponymous "Quiet American" of what is perhaps the most controversial novel of his career. Pyle is the brash young idealist sent out by Washington on a mysterious </span>
  <span id="freeText5363830880433433216" style="display:none"><b>Graham Greene's classic exploration of love, innocence, and morality in Vietnam</b><br /><br />"I never knew a man who had better motives for all the trouble he caused," Graham Greene's narrator Fowler remarks of Alden Pyle, the eponymous "Quiet American" of what is perhaps the most controversial novel of his career. Pyle is the brash young idealist sent out by Washington on a mysterious mission to Saigon, where the French Army struggles against the Vietminh guerrillas. As young Pyle's well-intentioned policies blunder into bloodshed, Fowler, a seasoned and cynical British reporter, finds it impossible to stand safely aside as an observer. But Fowler's motives for intervening are suspect, both to the police and himself, for Pyle has stolen Fowler's beautiful Vietnamese mistress.<br /><br />First published in 1956 and twice adapted to film, <i>The Quiet American</i> remains a terrifiying and prescient portrait of innocence at large. This Graham Greene Centennial Edition includes a new introductory essay by Robert Stone.</span>

<a href="/questions/155520-is-the-character-of-pyle-in-the-quiet">Is the character of Pyle in the Quiet American based on General Lansdale?</a>
</div>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/8079403-max"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1344194443i/8079403._UX100_CR0,6,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/8079403-max">Max</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">No. Lansdale was first stationed in Viet Nam in 1954. The events in the book took place in 1952. When Greene first met Lansdale he had nearly complete<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">â€¦more</a></span><span class="fullContent">No. Lansdale was first stationed in Viet Nam in 1954. The events in the book took place in 1952. When Greene first met Lansdale he had nearly completed the book. Also, Greene denied Lansdale was the basis for Alden Pyle. Pyle was based on Leo Hochstetter, public affairs director for Americaâ€™s Economic Aid Mission. Greene had shared a hotel room with him and they drove back to Saigon together just as Pyle and Fowler do in the book. On the way Hochstetler told Greene of the need for a third force and mentioned General Tháº¿ as a good choice. Greene did say that Hochstetter was much savvier than Pyle.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<a href="/questions/518598-is-the-book-better-than-movie-i-ve-seen">is the book better than movie?! I&#39;ve seen the movie and I love it so much, I wonder if the movie has removed many details from the book or not??</a>

<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/68200729-au-yong"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/68200729-au-yong">Au Yong</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">That partly depended on which movie version you were referring to and what you might have expected from the book.<br/>There were two movie versions, one ma<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">â€¦more</a></span><span class="fullContent">That partly depended on which movie version you were referring to and what you might have expected from the book.<br />There were two movie versions, one made in 1958 with Audie Murphy and Michael Redgrave in the leads; the other was released in 2002 with Michael Caine and Brendan Fraser.<br />We shall refer to the Michael Caine version in our reply.<br />Broadly speaking, the movie has not left out anything of material significance from the book. But like most movie versions, the producers will have to rearrange the sequence of events in the book.<br />The movie version starring Michael Caine and Brendan Fraser has done justice to Graham Greene's novel.<br />Perhaps the most memorable line in the movie was when there was an explosion which killed several civilian bystanders and the local Vietnamese guy said "You have to take sides - if you are to remain human."<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer744538600" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4564103208380401070"><i> I never knew a man who had better motives for all the trouble he caused</i>.<br/><br/>I assume that everyone for once in own life had to face such a moment that, though convinced about doing the right thing, felt nevertheless poorly and uncomfortably. How is it possible, we asked then, we acted righteously so why such bad feeling, such turbulence in our mind? We did a good choice so why this bile that fills our mouths? Why that need to rationalize our deeds? There was no other way, we say. But really? And th</span>
  <span id="freeText4564103208380401070" style="display:none"><i> I never knew a man who had better motives for all the trouble he caused</i>.<br /><br />I assume that everyone for once in own life had to face such a moment that, though convinced about doing the right thing, felt nevertheless poorly and uncomfortably. How is it possible, we asked then, we acted righteously so why such bad feeling, such turbulence in our mind? We did a good choice so why this bile that fills our mouths? Why that need to rationalize our deeds? There was no other way, we say. But really? And this is a feeling Graham Greene leaves me with almost every time. Something that still bothers and troubles me and pours cold water over me, my whole self - confidence and well-being.<br /><br />Because no matter how much we abide by our principles and sense of morality we constantly collide with the concept of the lesser evil and the common good, in short with situations that allow us or even encourage to justify our actions or omissions. And so is Thomas Fowler, somewhat cynical English reporter, for years residing in the East, whose life motto is to be not <i> engaged </i>. But is it really possibly to live without being involved? Thomas seems to care only for a few things in his life: willing body of young mistress in his bed and some pipes of opium to detach himself not only from the outside world, the whole thing takes place in the fifties during French-Vietnamese war, but also from own conscience. And one day in this more or less organized world enters the title quiet American, young and naÃ¯ve <i> impregnably armored by his good intentions and his ignorance </i> Alden Pyle, with his head stuffed with bookish knowledge and mouth packed with platitude on democracy and justice. He comes with his sense of mission to save the world and by the way of course falls in love with Fowlerâ€™s mistress.<br /><br />One could say that it is a banal situation only wrapped up in exotic costume and cast in some unusual scenery to add some spice. You couldn't be more wrong since this collision of East with West, old with new, that clash between youth and maturity, experience and naivety Greene played masterfully. And in the end neither youthful idealism of Pyle nor disillusioned stoicism of Fowler allows anyone to remain nonaligned. There is always that moment one must espouse whose side we are on. Because in fact, no matter how much we turn our eyes and try to stay neutral, even though we choose our own side, it is always a pure act of being irretrievably engaged.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14349848784995186477">This is a cautionary tale about the involvement of America and Britain in the Vietnam war. Reading this book was a great way to learn more about the Vietnam war.<br/><br/>The two main characters are symbols of the American and British participation in Vietnam. The British does not want to get involved in the war, and he is deluding himself that he is only an indifferent spectator. <br/><br/>Pyle, the American, represents the idealistic principles that the Americas brought in the Vietnam war and the lack of guilt fo</span>
  <span id="freeText14349848784995186477" style="display:none">This is a cautionary tale about the involvement of America and Britain in the Vietnam war. Reading this book was a great way to learn more about the Vietnam war.<br /><br />The two main characters are symbols of the American and British participation in Vietnam. The British does not want to get involved in the war, and he is deluding himself that he is only an indifferent spectator. <br /><br />Pyle, the American, represents the idealistic principles that the Americas brought in the Vietnam war and the lack of guilt for the damage they had created by their innocent causes. <br /><br />"Innocence is a kind of insanityâ€ â€œInnocence always calls mutely for protection when we would be so much wiser to guard ourselves against it: innocence is like a dumb leper who has lost his bell, wandering the world, meaning no harm.â€ <br /><br />One of the conclusions of the books is that â€œSooner or later...one has to take sides. If one is to remain human.â€ Another is somehow similar to the other book that I was reading in the same time, Blindness. It is about peoples's ignorance to human suffering, futility of life and the permanence of death. <br /><br />This is the first book I read by Greene and it won't be the last. I enjoyed his subtle tone. <br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1731431209" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1731431209" />
    <a title="Lizzy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30575403-lizzy"><img alt="Lizzy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512052362p2/30575403.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1731431209?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lizzy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lizzy" href="/user/show/30575403-lizzy">Lizzy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=classics-literay-fiction">classics-literay-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=stars-5">stars-5</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=favorites-of-all-times">favorites-of-all-times</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1731431209" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6342775963840167708"><i>
  <b>'I shut my eyes and she was again the same as she used to be: she was the hiss of steam, the clink of a cup, she was a certain hour of the night, and the promise of rest.'</b>
</i><br/><br/>Sometimes a few notable books cause me to start thinking just I turn the last page. So, excuse me for beginning this review with some of my latest ruminations. When I reflect on the meaning of life, although I am not a philosopher I do that sometimes, the fact that we are here for such a short while strikes me as so dismal.</span>
  <span id="freeText6342775963840167708" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>'I shut my eyes and she was again the same as she used to be: she was the hiss of steam, the clink of a cup, she was a certain hour of the night, and the promise of rest.'</b>
</i><br /><br />Sometimes a few notable books cause me to start thinking just I turn the last page. So, excuse me for beginning this review with some of my latest ruminations. When I reflect on the meaning of life, although I am not a philosopher I do that sometimes, the fact that we are here for such a short while strikes me as so dismal. If we are to have such a short life, how can we live it to the fullest?, I often ask myself. Our lives might be inconsequential, what are 70 or even 80 years compared with the history of humanity? And what is within our control? I hope to live up to my 80â€™s, but will I? As you might have guessed, I am an atheist and have no religion to alleviate my doubts. One thing I know is that there is always a beginning and an end. So I sometimes get melancholic by this musings. But how can we make the best of it? <br /><br />Then one day I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2533.Graham_Greene" title="Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">Graham Greene</a>â€™s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3698.The_Quiet_American" title="The Quiet American by Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">The Quiet American</a>, and everything makes sense again. Everything is suddenly all right. Oh, I am relieved not to have read it before, for now, it enthralled me for the first time. And probably tomorrow it will be another gem that will prove to me how right life is. And in reading it I convince myself that Greene shared a little in what I will call here my <i>contemplation moments</i>:<br /><i>
  <blockquote>From childhood I had never believed in permanence, and yet I had longed for it. Always I was afraid of losing happiness. This month, next year... If not next year, in three years. Death was the only absolute value in my world. Lose life and one would lose nothing again forever. I envied those who could believe in a God and I distrusted them. I felt they were keeping their courage up with a fable of the changeless and the permanent. Death was far more certain than God, and with death there would be no longer the daily possibility of love dying.</blockquote>
</i><br />What is <i>The Quiet American</i> all about, after all? Many see it as a mere allegory between the forces involved in the conflict portrayed by Greene. Others recognize it as just a war or spy novel. I prefer to read it as a love triangle where Fowley, the surly middle-aged British war correspondent; and Pyle, the young and naÃ¯ve American spy; dispute the love and possession of the young and beautiful Vietnamese Phuong. Their tangled relationship could, of course, stand for the intricacies of the conflict that was ravaging Viet Nam and what was yet to come. But letâ€™s just leave at that.<br /><br />Like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29641.The_End_of_the_Affair" title="The End of the Affair by Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">The End of the Affair</a>, this is a Greene novel that affects you viscerally. A naÃ¯ve American CIA operative, fresh from Yale, arrives in Vietnam and promptly steals the narrator's lover then gets himself and several Vietnamese killed. That seems a pretty straightforward plot. But it is far from it. The story goes back and forth in time and thus increasingly reveals all that is brewing inside the protagonist and narrator Fowler. From the beginning, he felt protective of Pyke, and despite their dispute over the girl, they remain quite good friends:<br /><i>
  <blockquote>â€œI stopped our trishaw outside the Chalet and said to Phuong, â€œGo in and find a table. I had better look after Pyle.â€ That was my first instinct â€“ to protect him. It never occurred to me that there was greater need to protect myself. Innocence always calls mutely for protection when we would be so much wiser to guard ourselves against it; innocence is like a dumb leper who has lost his bell, wandering the world, meaning no harm.â€</blockquote>
</i><br />But the triangle of Fowler, Pyle and Phuong was even more intricate. That is the beauty of it, how they dealt with each other and their desires openly:<br /><i>
  <blockquote>"What can you offer her?" he asked me with anger. "A couple hundred dollars when you leave for England, or will you pass her on with the furniture?"<br />"The furniture isn't mine."<br />"She is not either. Phuong, will you marry me?"<br />"What about the blood group?" I said. "And a health certificate. You'll need hers, surely? Maybe you ought to have mine too. And her horoscope - no, that is an Indian custom."<br />"Will you marry me?"<br />"Say it in French," I said. "I'm damned if I'll interpret for you anymore."</blockquote>
</i><br />With each word, each line and each paragraph Greene demonstrates, through his protagonist, how he articulates internal struggles exceptionally well: the meditating but judgmental mind, the bustling innermost turmoil. He articulates like few the dark side of love â€“ the narrator with his quick and keen mind; with a fastidious view of the world, and a hardened heart. Despite death, anguish, and hardship, there is hope in the end. And each sentence is a treasure, making it a pleasure to quote Greene.<br /><br />And we first hear of Fowlerâ€™s cynical views:<br /><i>
  <blockquote>"To be in love is to see yourself as someone else sees you, it is to be in love with the falsified and exalted image of yourself. In love we are incapable of honour â€“ the courageous act is no more than playing a part to an audience of two. Perhaps I was no longer in love but I remembered."</blockquote>
</i><br />And how he always preferred escape to the possibility of loss:<br /><i>
  <blockquote>"No, that woman came earlier. When I left my wife."<br />"What happened?"<br />"I left her, too."<br />"Why?"<br />Why indeed? "We are fools," I said, "when we love. I was terrified of losing her. I thought I saw her changing â€“ I don't know if she really was, but I couldn't bear the uncertainty any longer. I ran towards the finish just as a coward runs towards the enemy and wins a medal. I wanted to get death over." <br />"Death?"<br />"It was a kind of death. And I came east."</blockquote>
</i><br />Thus, when Pyke saves his life, Fowler was far from thankful, for he had been looking for exactly that escape when he came to Saigon:<br /><i>
  <blockquote>"We've made it," Pyle said, and even in my pain I wondered what we'd made: for me, old age, an editor's chair, loneliness; and for him, one knows now that he spoke prematurely. Then in the cold, we settled down to wait. Along the road a bonfire burst into life: it burnt merrily like a celebration. "That's my car," I said.</blockquote>
</i><br />All through Greeneâ€™s narrative, we catch glimpses of how his protagonist recognizes that love, even for the toughest of them, brings with it the possibility of pain. <i>'Her pain struck at my pain: we were back at the old routine of hurting each other. If only it were possible to love without injury â€“ fidelity isn't enough.'</i> But despite knowing from experience the damage of loss, the pain was not any less. <i>'I wish I could have those nights back. I'm still in love, Pyle, and I'm a wasting asset.'</i> But there was more to it, even if less hurtful. <i>'Oh, and there was pride, of course. It takes a long time before we cease to feel proud of being wanted.'</i> But our protagonist fights all that he believes to be true. However, he cannot escape; to comprehend it all seems impossible and a recurring theme with Greene. <i>'Wouldn't we all do better not trying to understand, accepting the fact that no human being will ever understand another, not a wife a husband, a lover a mistress?'</i> It is unforgivably poignant in its sufferings. <i>'I began to plan the life I had still somehow to live and to remember memories in order to eliminate them. Happy memories are the worst, and I tried to remember the unhappy.'</i> Above all, these passages touch us all the more and never go away for their flashes of beauty.<br /><br />Thus, the story of the quiet American is the story of idealism, of naivetÃ©, with not knowing but supposing to know best. Pyleâ€™s good intentions may seem endearing, but they come from ignorance, and Fowler recognizes from the beginning how dangerous, and guesses that his fate was so sealed. <br /><i>
  <blockquote>"Have you any hunch," he asked, "why they killed him? and who?"<br />Suddenly I was angry; I was tired of the whole pack of them with their private stores of Coca-Cola and their portable hospitals and their wide cars and their not quite latest guns. I said, "Yes. They killed him because he was too innocent to live. He was young and ignorant and silly to get involved.â€</blockquote>
</i><br />Despite the fact that some can view the Vietnamese as weak and ignorant, Fowler by the end presents us the reality of Phuong, and for me, it could not be more honest, for these are people that in their effort to survive have to make the most of what they have. Talking to Pyke, when they discuss Phuongâ€™s future we read how well he can judge her:<br /><i>
  <blockquote>"She's no child. She is tougher than you'll ever be. Do you know the kind of polish that doesn't take scratches? That's Phuong. She can survive a dozen of us. She'll get old, that's all. She'll suffer from childbirth and hunger and cold and rheumatism, but she'll never suffer like we do from thought, obsession-she won't scratch, she'll only decay."</blockquote>
</i><br />One of the dilemmas that Fowler faces since arriving in Viet Nam is that he avoided getting involved. He was always the mere correspondent, just relaying the news he imagined his editors would want to see published. He had an assistant that was his way of staying distant. <br /><i>
  <blockquote>"'You can rule me out,' I said. 'I'm not involved. Not involved,' I repeated. It had been an article of my creed. The human condition being what it was, let them fight, let them love, let them murder, I would not be involved. My fellow journalists called themselves correspondents; I preferred the title of reporter. I wrote what I saw. I took no action â€“ even an opinion is a kind of action."</blockquote>
</i><br />But what he discovered is that frequently we do not have the luxury of staying neutral. Fowler realizes late in his affair that he loved Phuong, also discover that life sometimes has its way of forcing on us to make a stand. <br /><i>
  <blockquote>Suffering is not increased by numbers: one body can contain all the suffering the world can feel. I had judged like a journalist in terms of quantity and I betrayed my own principle; I had become as engaged as Pyle, and it seemed to me that no decision would ever be simple again.</blockquote>
</i> <br />I loved The Quiet American. It has everything that a discerning reader might want in a book. An astute glimpse of American foreign policy, depicted as <i> a dumb leper who has lost his bell, wandering the world, meaning no harm.</i> It has a credible and fascinating love triangle set in a hazy and exotic setting. It has challenging characters uncovered as thoroughly human with all their imperfections. All this delivered through a majestic narrative. This novel will grab hold of you; and by the end, as with most great examples of literature, you'll be a little wiser and more enchanted with life because of it.<br />___<br /><i>I want to thank my friend Vessey for granting me the privilege of reading The Quiet American with her.</i></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11108582606085903856">An absolutely brilliant book. I think it is a genuine masterpiece to be enjoyed on numerous different levels. It goes straight to my favorites ever list! Graham Greene employs the right tone for this book, cynical yet compassionate.   <span id="freeText11108582606085903856" style="display:none">An absolutely brilliant book. I think it is a genuine masterpiece to be enjoyed on numerous different levels. It goes straight to my favorites ever list! Graham Greene employs the right tone for this book, cynical yet compassionate. Correspondent Fowler's non-commitment is the best attitude for the place and time of his assignment in Vietnam, but whether it is psychologically healthy cannot be said with certainty. Written in 1955, it is shocking to see how very relevant the book still is today, perhaps even increasingly so. The ignorance and misconception of the stereotypical American proves to be truly deadly. In his ignorant fanatism CIA agent Pyle stays convinced of his good intentions, even when witnessing with his own eyes the devastation he brings about. I thought a few times that the saying: â€œFools rush in where angels fear to treadâ€ was very applicable. And that is still applicable in the world today. A pity that this book was not a mandatory book to be read by the U.S. military when sending the first troops to Vietnam halfway the 1960s. Might have warned them for a hopeless endeavour.<br />I am so impressed by this book and decided to read more Graham Greene novels this year. It was purely coincidental that I read Greene for the first time when reading 'The End of the Affair' with its annoying catholicism and that is exactly what kept me from reading his other novels. I found out that this was just a phase in his life which he abandoned after he divorced his very catholic wife.</span>

<div id="review_3034005038" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3034005038" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3034005038?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780679600145" href="/book/show/828083.The_Quiet_American">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=politics">politics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=vietnam">vietnam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=1950s">1950s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=spies">spies</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3034005038" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4128591679915485066"><b>â€œThat was my first instinct -- to protect him. It never occurred to me that there was a greater need to protect myself. Innocence always calls mutely for protection when we would be so much wiser to guard ourselves against it: innocence is like a dumb leper who has lost his bell, wandering the world, meaning no harm.â€</b><br/><br/>As if this book were not brilliant enough for its multi-layered plot and meaningful, carefully written prose, it was also a harbinger of the disaster awaiting the United State's inv</span>
  <span id="freeText4128591679915485066" style="display:none"><b>â€œThat was my first instinct -- to protect him. It never occurred to me that there was a greater need to protect myself. Innocence always calls mutely for protection when we would be so much wiser to guard ourselves against it: innocence is like a dumb leper who has lost his bell, wandering the world, meaning no harm.â€</b><br /><br />As if this book were not brilliant enough for its multi-layered plot and meaningful, carefully written prose, it was also a harbinger of the disaster awaiting the United State's involvement in Vietnam. If policy makers would have read this book and realized that they were consulting an oracle, many unnecessary deaths would have been avoided; many lives would have never been shattered, and the billions of dollars spent trying to destroy a mythical idea could have been spent advancing humanity in numerous more productive ways. <br /><br />Instead of being lauded as a cautionary tale, the book was declared anti-American.<br /><br />Alden Pyle, not the typical blustering, pushy American abroad but a rather quiet American, was in Vietnam. He wasnâ€™t there to learn about the culture. He was there to figure out the best way to impose his Western point of view on a country in turmoil. He had been fully indoctrinated into the idea of American Exceptionalism. This was a strength because believing in oneself and a cause is essential to achieving success, but it was also a weakness because it potentially keeps an American from recognizing what has gone wrong for others will also go wrong for them. It also keeps an American from seeing the value in a foreign culture and that the concepts of others of what makes a wonderful life may be completely different from what Americans are being led to believe is an exceptional way of life. <br /><br />Alden Pyle, in other words, was a very dangerous man. <br /><br />Thomas Fowler was a world weary British journalist, addicted to opium, and living with a 20 year old Vietnamese woman named Phuong. There were some reverential descriptions by Fowler about his relationship with opium and Phuong dutifully serving him his pipe in a manner reminiscent of Japanese tea ceremonies. <i>â€It was a superstition among them that a lover who smoked would always return, even from France. A manâ€™s sexual capacity might be injured by smoking, but they would always prefer a faithful to a potent lover. Now she was kneading the little ball of hot paste on the convex margin of the bowl and I could smell the opium. There is no smell like it.â€</i> <br /><br />Fowler had found a simple way of life that made him way happier than I think he ever expected to be. He had a job that he understood. He had a reasonably nice apartment. He had a beautiful girlfriend who provided him with the comfort of companionship and sexual gratification. He didnâ€™t need anything more than this. <br /><br />With the arrival of Pyle, this nirvana existence was suddenly in jeopardy. Pyle became enamored with Phuong. Fowler had a dilemma which Pyle soon exploited in his quest to â€œsaveâ€ Phuong from the lecherous clutches of this old world colonizer. I hadnâ€™t really thought about it until this reading, but Pyleâ€™s need to save Phuong was symbolic of the American belief that Europe was corrupt and only America could guide the world forward. Fowler was British and the French colonized Vietnam, but the Brits were the largest, â€œmost successfulâ€ colonizers the world had ever seen.<br /><br />Come with me little girl. I am pure of heart. <br /><br />Fowlerâ€™s dilemma was a serious disadvantage, given that he was already married to a devoted Catholic woman back in England who did not want to divorce him. Fowlerâ€™s poignant letters to his wife to try and change her mind were revealing about his true feelings about Phuong. The reader might wonder if losing Phuong was just an inconvenience or he really did love her. <i>â€Perhaps you will believe when I tell you that to lose her will be, for me, the beginning of death.â€</i> Fowlerâ€™s years of marriage had left he and his wife scarred and battered. A mere prick by one to the other would now bleed as heavily as a mortal wound. <br /><br />Pyle was irritatingly trying to play fair in the tug of war over Phuong. Fowler had no such illusions about playing fair. He definitely subscribed to the adage, â€œAll is fair in love and war.â€ This is Vietnam in the midst of a long struggle and love is always more poignant against the backdrop of war. Phuongâ€™s more practical sister wanted her to go with Pyle because he was free to marry her. I kept thinking to myself as this love triangle unfolded that the one person whom we really didnâ€™t know her feelings was Phuong. Fowler at several points accused Pyle of treating Phuong like a child, which was true. To Pyle, she was a mere child who must be saved from her circumstances. He was the white knight and sitting so high on his horse that one might wonder if he really wanted her or simply wanted her away from Fowler. The American Imperialist knew best. Out with the old and in with the new. <br /><br />How far would Fowler go to win this battle with Pyle? By the end of the book, you will see. <br /><br />The frustrating thing for Fowler was that he liked Pyle, and Pyle, despite his misgivings about Fowler, liked him as well. It is so much easier when our adversaries are asshats with few redeeming qualities. We can feel vindicated in our all consuming loathing of them. Under different circumstances, Fowler and Pyle might have been lifelong friends, but there were other things percolating that would keep them from being friends. What exactly was Pyle up to in Vietnam? And what did he mean about all this blathering about creating a third force? Fowler, in the course of his job, would have crippled the budding friendship by eventually revealing the truth, so alas, there really was no chance for Pyle and Fowler to walk off into the sunset together, conversing about the beginning of a beautiful friendship.<br /><br />Still I would want to ask Pyle, out of all the beautiful women in all of Vietnam, you had to pick mine?<br /><br />So you can read this book at whatever depth you chose and still find it to be one of the best books youâ€™ve ever read. I do believe this is my third read, and I made new connections and observations that I hadnâ€™t with the previous two reads. It is such a powerful story for such a short book, proving that epic tales donâ€™t have to come in whale size packages. <br /><br />I want to thank my friend Lisa Lieberman for prompting this latest reading of <i>The Quiet American</i>. Her new book <i>The Glass Forest</i> is a tribute to Graham Greeneâ€™s novel. I have been wanting to reread <i>The Quiet American</i> for some time now, and her book release was the perfect excuse.<br /><br />Lisa is giving away a free ebook of her first book, <i>All the Wrong Places</i> for the month of November as a lead up to the release of <i>The Glass Forest</i> on December 10th. Donâ€™t miss out! Click this link to get your free book! <a href="https://dl.bookfunnel.com/md4n72as3y" rel="nofollow noopener">All the Wrong Places Free eBook</a> You must discover for yourself why I call her the Queen of the Hollywood Noir. <br /><br /> If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br />I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>

<div id="review_3064006311" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3064006311" />
    <a title="Fergus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus"><img alt="Fergus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567548184p2/70395042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3064006311?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fergus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fergus" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus">Fergus</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer743646556112159613">The other day it occurred to me that this jarringly complex book was a wry attempt on Graham Greeneâ€™s part to defrock the dreaded americanos of The Power and Glory with an ironic image - the gregarious, wellmeaning, crewcut volunteers of that brave invention, the Peace Corps. <br/><br/>Greene was always so perplexed by the bizarre and continually morphing forms of violent behaviour in the world - and stymied by the fact that so many of the cleared paths open to it are paved by bystandersâ€™ like Pyleâ€™s inno</span>
  <span id="freeText743646556112159613" style="display:none">The other day it occurred to me that this jarringly complex book was a wry attempt on Graham Greeneâ€™s part to defrock the dreaded americanos of The Power and Glory with an ironic image - the gregarious, wellmeaning, crewcut volunteers of that brave invention, the Peace Corps. <br /><br />Greene was always so perplexed by the bizarre and continually morphing forms of violent behaviour in the world - and stymied by the fact that so many of the cleared paths open to it are paved by bystandersâ€™ like Pyleâ€™s innocent good intentions - that he became more and more obsessively predisposed to a sort of truculent silence, a retreat into his own less than virtuous anodynes.<br /><br />He agreed wholeheartedly, at a rather morbid distance from orthodoxy, with T.S. Eliotâ€™s image of the beleaguered and battered Word of God at the non-retaliative heart of existence:<br /><br />We would see a sign!<br />The Word within the world<br />Unable to speak a word<br />Swaddled in darkness.<br />Signs are taken for wonders!<br />Against the Word <br />The unstilled world still whirled<br />About the Centre of the Silent Word.<br /><br />The image of the crucifixion is central to the Catholic imagination, and Greene was imbued with it too.<br /><br />So, drugged and dropped-out in his down-for-the-count habits, like the principal character of this novel - who is a world-weary opportunist - he distances the innocence of Pyle in much the same way as John Keats stylistically distances his own too-Personal experiences through the romance of literate and storied rhyme.<br /><br />For Keatsâ€™ discovery of the literary device of distancing - you can see it in the mythical sense of chivalrous historicity imparted to a rather unworthily mundane act, in his St Agnesâ€™ Eve - gave him a methodology to â€œglean (a collection of poetic images, jostling for their release from) his teeming brain;â€ AND as well to provide him with a catharsis of his own nagging sins in public â€˜confession.â€™<br /><br />So too, Greene â€˜distancesâ€™ his own perverse personas in the manic phases of his bipolar disorder, AND gives voice to his personal mistrust of the US presence in Vietnam in one fell swoop: by creating the Quiet American, Pyle.<br /><br />My third-year uni prof said distancing came to the foreground with the lyrical images of Wordsworth, notably in his ennobling segue from the sight of a young peasant girl working in the fields into a meditative digression into a timeless and and spaceless apotheosis of her lyricism, in The Solitary Reaper.<br /><br />Itâ€™s a long, grim sludge for us, though, from the wheat fields of pastoral England to the napalmed black jungle of Vietnam.<br /><br />The Quiet American is likewise, for Greene, the distancing of his own sordid presence amid the horrific American aporia of innocent intentions gone so abominably wrong, and the making of Pyle into a universal symbol as well as a fractured piece of himself as a great writer.<br /><br />Mishaps dog the young Pyle in Vietnam.<br /><br />As they did his country after a Quiet America entered its jungles.<br /><br />For the young America had indeed entered Quietly into French Indochina - but would leave it injured and aged.<br /><br />But the damaging self-inflicted wounds of its bungled innocence would prove long lasting.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2643797058208922460">499. The Quiet American, Graham Greene (1904-1992)<br/><br/>The Quiet American is a 1955 novel by English author Graham Greene which depicts French colonialism in Vietnam being uprooted by the Americans during the 1950s. The novel implicitly questions the foundations of growing American involvement in Vietnam in the 1950s and is unique in its exploration of the subject topic through the links among its three main characters - Fowler, Pyle and Phuong. <br/><br/>The novel has received much attention due to its predic</span>
  <span id="freeText2643797058208922460" style="display:none">499. The Quiet American, Graham Greene (1904-1992)<br /><br />The Quiet American is a 1955 novel by English author Graham Greene which depicts French colonialism in Vietnam being uprooted by the Americans during the 1950s. The novel implicitly questions the foundations of growing American involvement in Vietnam in the 1950s and is unique in its exploration of the subject topic through the links among its three main characters - Fowler, Pyle and Phuong. <br /><br />The novel has received much attention due to its prediction of the outcome of the Vietnam War and subsequent American foreign policy since the 1950s. Graham Greene portrays a U.S. official named Pyle as so blinded by American exceptionalism that he cannot see the calamities he brings upon the Vietnamese. It was adapted as two different movies, one in 1958 and another in 2002. <br /><br />The book uses Greene's experiences as a war correspondent for The Times and Le Figaro in French Indochina 1951â€“1954. He was apparently inspired to write The Quiet American during October 1951 while driving back to Saigon from Ben Tre province.         <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer5265589680212455563">This is an amazing story about the French colonial war in Vietnam and an incompetent CIA-wanna be agent seen through the eyes of a opium-addicted British journalist. Cynicism abounds. Great writing, gripping scenes. Excellent read. A true classic. I definitely need to read more Graham Greene.<br/><br/>If you enjoyed this book and wish to have more background on the historical canvas on which the story was painted, I highly suggest Fredrik Logevall's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2006959648?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Embers of War</a> about the French Indochina War and Frances</span>
  <span id="freeText5265589680212455563" style="display:none">This is an amazing story about the French colonial war in Vietnam and an incompetent CIA-wanna be agent seen through the eyes of a opium-addicted British journalist. Cynicism abounds. Great writing, gripping scenes. Excellent read. A true classic. I definitely need to read more Graham Greene.<br /><br />If you enjoyed this book and wish to have more background on the historical canvas on which the story was painted, I highly suggest Fredrik Logevall's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2006959648?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Embers of War</a> about the French Indochina War and Frances Fitzgerald's masterful <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2028184089" rel="nofollow noopener">Fire in the Lake</a> about the Vietnamese and the American conflict. Gripping stories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5265589680212455563" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer403361821135381808">â€œI canâ€™t say what made me fall in love with Vietnam - that a womanâ€™s voice can drug you; that everything is so intense.   <span id="freeText403361821135381808" style="display:none">â€œI canâ€™t say what made me fall in love with Vietnam - that a womanâ€™s voice can drug you; that everything is so intense. The colors, the taste, even the rain. Nothing like the filthy rain in London. The smell: thatâ€™s the first thing that hits you, promising everything in exchange for your soul. You could be forgiven for thinking there was no war; that the gunshots were fireworks; that only pleasure matters. A pipe of opium, or the touch of a girl who might tell you she loves you. And then, something happens, as you knew it would. And nothing can ever be the same again.â€ <br /><br />Oh, I loved rereading this book, which I originally read so long ago I had largely forgotten it. Itâ€™s a story narrated by English journalist Thomas Fowler about the end of French colonialism in Vietnam and the beginning of American involvement there leading to the Vietnam War. Against the political background of a country used as a punching bag by France, Japan, then France again, then the US, is a love triangle between the older Fowler, a young Texas CIA agent named Alden Pyle, and Phuong, a young Vietnamese woman.<br /><br />â€œI shut my eyes and she was again the same as she used to be: she was the hiss of steam, the clink of a cup, she was a certain hour of the night and the promise of rest.â€<br /><br />The novel, written in 1952-55 and based on Greeneâ€™s own work as a war correspondent in Indochina from 1951-54, harshly critiques the American involvement in Vietnam in the fifties and uses the three main characters to in part make this critique. Pyle, the â€œquiet American,â€ canâ€™t properly see the implications of what they are doing in that country, prefiguring the terrible, ignorant things that would happen there in the decades to come.<br /><br />The â€œdomino theoryâ€ that was the foundation of the US policy (that if Vietnam is lost to communism then all the others in the area would then fall) is seen in this dialogue between Fowler and Kyle:<br /><br />"They don't want communism." [Kyle, the American]<br />"They want enough rice," I [Fowler] said. "They don't want to be shot at. They want one day to be much the same as another. They don't want our white skins around telling them what they want."<br />"If Indochina goes--"<br />"I know that record. Siam goes. Malaya goes. Indonesia goes. What does 'go' mean? If I believed in your God and another life, I'd bet my future harp against your golden crown that in five hundred years there may be no New York or London, but they'll be growing paddy in these fields, they'll be carrying their produce to market on long poles, wearing their pointed hats. The small boys will still be sitting on their buffaloes.â€<br /><br />Fowler is a cynic; â€œI was a correspondent: I thought in headlines;â€ heâ€™s an atheist, he hates politics, and hates nation states that arrogantly pursue colonialist takeovers, so in response he pretends to not care: <br /><br />â€œâ€™I'm not involved, not involved,â€™ I repeated. It has been an article of my creed. The human condition being what it was, let them fight, let them love, let them murder, I would not be involved. My fellow journalists called themselves correspondents; I preferred the title of reporter. I wrote what I saw. I took no action â€“ even an opinion is a kind of action.â€ <br /><br />â€œâ€™Ah,â€ he [a Vietnamese friend] said, â€œBut you will be involved. You will all be involved some day.â€<br /><br />And so we were involved! And still are, in a sense, in making decisions about who on the planet gets protected and fed and who do not.<br /> <br />In the end Fowler does take a stand, raging to Pyle about a needless and horrific bombing incident â€œorchestratedâ€ by the Americans that causes many civilian casualties. But itâ€™s a novel, not a political tract. Itâ€™s an often powerfully written book that helps see colonialism in a personal context. Fowler wants Phuong, Pyle wants her, too; but what does she really want? It kind of reminded me in that respect of another great post-colonialist book by J. M. Coetzee, Waiting for the Barbarians, that sees colonialism and sexism as two aspects of the same condition.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18005988546336634173"><i>"She was the hiss of steam, the clink of a cup, she was a certain hour of the night and the promise of rest."</i><br/><br/>Graham Greene`s The Quiet American is <b>a wonderful piece of literature</b>. It's partly love story, partly war and courage story, partly suffering and sad story, partly a story of repent and partly a story filled with selfishness.<br/><br/><i>Do read the below lines to get a feel of what this book is about, and then read this book.</i> <br/><br/>"Death was the only absolute value in my world. Lose life and one would los</span>
  <span id="freeText18005988546336634173" style="display:none"><i>"She was the hiss of steam, the clink of a cup, she was a certain hour of the night and the promise of rest."</i><br /><br />Graham Greene`s The Quiet American is <b>a wonderful piece of literature</b>. It's partly love story, partly war and courage story, partly suffering and sad story, partly a story of repent and partly a story filled with selfishness.<br /><br /><i>Do read the below lines to get a feel of what this book is about, and then read this book.</i> <br /><br />"Death was the only absolute value in my world. Lose life and one would lose nothing again for ever."<br /><br />"Wouldn't we all do better not trying to understand, accepting the fact that no human being will ever understand another, not a wife a husband, a lover a mistress, nor a parent a child? Perhaps that's why men have invited God - a being capable of understanding."<br /><br />"With a return ticket courage becomes an intellectual exercise, like a monk`s flagellation."<br /><br />"To be in love is to see yourself as someone else sees you, it is to be in love with the falsified and exalted image of yourself. In love we are incapable of honour."<br /><br />"I know myself, and I know the depth of my selfishness. I cannot be at ease if someone else is in pain, visibly or audibly or factually. Sometimes this is mistaken by the innocent for unselfishness, when all I was doing is sacrificing a small good - in this case postponement in attending to my hurt - for the sake of a far greater good, a peace of mind when I need think only of myself."<br /><br />"In the moment of shock there is little pain..."<br /><br />"Suffering is not increased by numbers : one body can contain all the suffering the world can feel." <br /><br /><b>"Everything had gone right with me since he had died, but how I wished there existed someone to whom I could say that I was sorry."</b></span>

<div id="review_3412669321" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3412669321" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7202176-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1324164999p2/7202176.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3412669321?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/7202176-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/39215986-the-quiet-american">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=1950s">1950s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7202176-tim?shelf=classic">classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3412669321" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6700221792227691518"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1594075826i/29771964.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>I don't know how to review this book. I cannot fairly review it as my own notion of the book is so altered by my own experiences, that I'm not sure anyone reading it would see what I see. Hell, to a certain extent, I know they can't... not quite the same at least. <br/><br/>Instead I will try to express how this book made me feel.<br/><br/>This book took me way too long to read. Not because it was bad, life just kept getting in my way even when I would much rather have been reading, that said, I was happy to take </span>
  <span id="freeText6700221792227691518" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1594075826i/29771964.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>I don't know how to review this book. I cannot fairly review it as my own notion of the book is so altered by my own experiences, that I'm not sure anyone reading it would see what I see. Hell, to a certain extent, I know they can't... not quite the same at least. <br><br>Instead I will try to express how this book made me feel.<br><br>This book took me way too long to read. Not because it was bad, life just kept getting in my way even when I would much rather have been reading, that said, I was happy to take my time. This book transported me back to a different place. I've been to Vietnam multiple times, it's where my wife is from and it's one of the most beautiful countries I've ever seen. I don't like traveling much personally, and the flight there is long (over 24 hours counting layovers), but I always find it worth it when we go back.<br><br>This book took me back there at a much cheaper price. Vietnam has changed a lot in the 65 years since Greene wrote this book... but in so many way it is still the same. Reading it, I was reminded of hot nights in Saigon (where the temperature was almost always 90+ degrees and the occasional bursts of rain were always a relief despite how heavy the downpours got). I was reminded of the architecture, the clothing, the traffic and smells. <br><br>The plot is interesting, and one might be surprised to know it was written before America really got into the Vietnam war (Greene's insight into how America operated is sadly only too true), and the book comes off melancholic for multiple reasons which Greene did not necessarily intend at the time. This is one of those books that was no doubt very good when published, but honestly time has made better. It becomes both a good story and a sad warning about mistakes that were made, and how one should avoid them. <br><br>The prose is beautiful and much of the dialogue quite amusing. We have a delightfully sarcastic narrator, and many of the people he meets takes a similar cynical tone. My favorite bit of dialogue is when someone questions a reporter about a news briefing in Hanoi:<br><br>"There's a rumor that the Vietminh have broken into Phat Diem, burned the Cathedral, chased out the Bishop."<br><br>"They wouldn't tell us about that in Hanoi. That's not a victory." <br><br>Do I suggest the book? Absolutely. Even without the personal experiences, this is an extremely good book. For me though... it's going on my favorites shelf. 5/5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6700221792227691518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_637368025" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer14265668389081020507">I donâ€™t know. I guess this is what you would get if you crossed Ernest Hemingway with John le CarrÃ©? Maybe.<br/><br/><i>The Quiet American</i> is the story of a British journalist covering the war in 1950s French Indochina and the annoying American who disrupts his complacent lifestyle. Sure, heâ€™s in a war zone, but he has never had it better. He has a beautiful girl by his side and he finds it possible to remain relatively safe, both physically and emotionallyâ€”physically in that most of his reporting duties occ</span>
  <span id="freeText14265668389081020507" style="display:none">I donâ€™t know. I guess this is what you would get if you crossed Ernest Hemingway with John le CarrÃ©? Maybe.<br /><br /><i>The Quiet American</i> is the story of a British journalist covering the war in 1950s French Indochina and the annoying American who disrupts his complacent lifestyle. Sure, heâ€™s in a war zone, but he has never had it better. He has a beautiful girl by his side and he finds it possible to remain relatively safe, both physically and emotionallyâ€”physically in that most of his reporting duties occur behind the front lines; and emotionally in that he is able to convince himself of Britainâ€™s (and by extension <i>his</i>) lack of involvement with the war itself. It is <i>their</i> problem.<br /><br />The annoying American changes all that, and it seems to me the Americanâ€”Pyle, by nameâ€”is presented by Greene as an extension of America itself: innocent in its idealistic principles, yet dangerous in its perceived lack of guilt for the harm its idealism causes.<br /><br />Without getting into specifics, there is more to Pyle than meets the eye, and even though I did not love this story as much as I had hoped to, it did pull together for me in the end. The protagonist learns that at some point, it becomes necessary to make a choice and become involved no matter how adamantly you believe in your own neutrality. Greene does write with a remarkable degree of restraintâ€”in my opinion, his writing could even be said to be <i>too</i> restrainedâ€”which is how he packs a story with such complexity into a reasonable number of pages. The problem I had with it lay mostly in the fact that I did not experience much of a connection with any of the charactersâ€™ relationships. Though Pyle and Fowler essentially fight over a girl, it is a girl whom neither of them seems to really love, and the two men themselves donâ€™t actually develop any quantifiable bond of friendship that would have otherwise made the story more affecting.<br /><br />Anyway, I am not done with Graham Greene yet. Some guy on Goodreads named Ben told me to read <i>The End of the Affair</i>, so I suppose that is what Iâ€™ll go ahead and do next.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer75204607" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer9402266075740305520">"<i>War and Love -- they have always been compared.</i>"<br/> <br/>Like <i>The End of the Affair</i>, this is a Greene novel that affects you viscerally. It is a war novel, set in Vietnam. Being so, it is not cheerful or pretty: dead children lying in the street and the like. It hits on the complexities of war; the complexity of morals: how it's impossible to stay neutral forever on such matters when youâ€™re directly involved: you have to make a decision: you must decide, or you're as good as dead.<br/><br/><i>"'You can rule me out,</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9402266075740305520" style="display:none">"<i>War and Love -- they have always been compared.</i>"<br /> <br />Like <i>The End of the Affair</i>, this is a Greene novel that affects you viscerally. It is a war novel, set in Vietnam. Being so, it is not cheerful or pretty: dead children lying in the street and the like. It hits on the complexities of war; the complexity of morals: how it's impossible to stay neutral forever on such matters when youâ€™re directly involved: you have to make a decision: you must decide, or you're as good as dead.<br /><br /><i>"'You can rule me out,' I said. 'I'm not involved. Not involved,' I repeated. It had been an article of my creed. The human condition being what it was, let them fight, let them love, let them murder, I would not be involved. My fellow journalists called themselves correspondents; I preferred the title of reporter. I wrote what I saw. I took no action -- even an opinion is a kind of action."</i> <br /> <br />It's something our protagonist fights, and itâ€™s a recurring theme: <i>"Wouldn't we all do better not trying to understand, accepting the fact that no human being will ever understand another, not a wife a husband, a lover a mistress, nor a parent a child? Perhaps that's why men have invented God -- a being capable of understanding. Perhaps if I wanted to be understood or to understand I would bam-boozle myself into belief, but I am a reporter; God exists only for leader-writers."</i><br /> <br />Greene has proven to me that he articulates internal struggles exceptionally well: the ruminating but judgmental mind, the bustling churnings of the inside. And Iâ€™m not sure <i>anybody</i> articulates the dark side of love better than he does. Our protagonist here has a quick mind; cold but keen, with an accurate view of the world, and a hardened heart.<br /><br /><i>"To be in love is to see yourself as someone else sees you, it is to be in love with the falsified and exalted image of yourself. In love we are incapable of honour -- the courageous act is no more than playing a part to an audience of two. Perhaps I was no longer in love but I remembered."</i><br /> <br />But you see, love even makes the toughest of us feel pain.<br /><br /><i>"Her pain struck at my pain: we were back at the old routine of hurting each other. If only it were possible to love without injury -- fidelity isn't enough: I had been faithful to Anne and yet I had injured her. The hurt is in the act of possession: we are too small in mind and body to possess another person without pride or to be possessed without humiliation."</i><br /> <br />These quotes bite because they're so true. They are painful and <i>real</i>.<br /> <br /><i>"I wish I could have those nights back. I'm still in love, Pyle, and I'm a wasting asset. Oh, and there was pride, of course. It takes a long time before we cease to feel proud of being wanted. Though God knows why we should feel it, when we look around and see who is wanted too."</i><br /> <br />But the beauty of it; it's still there; it doesn't fully go away; and these flashes of beauty live in the memory forever:<br /> <br /><i>"I shut my eyes and she was again the same as she used to be: she was the hiss of steam, the clink of a cup, she was a certain hour of the night, and the promise of rest."</i><br /> <br />Oh yeah, and there's plenty of intellectual heft to this, too: the story of the quiet American is a discerning model -- a microcosm if you will -- of the United State's idealism with respect to Vietnam. Greene pulls this off in a clever fashion: Pyleâ€™s good intentions are endearing, even admirable at times -- but they are also ignorant, and therefore dangerous.<br /> <br />This novel will grab hold of you; and by the end -- as with most good pieces of literature -- you'll be a little more worldly and wiser because of it.<br /></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer960008302" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1495169261512482199">I was pleasantly surprised how moving this story was and how strongly I warmed up to the humanity of the main character in the face of his generally detached outlook. Thomas Fowler is in a slump. As a British war correspondent working out of Saigon in French-occupied Vietnam, he gets a daily dose of duplicity and brutality in the world of ongoing guerilla conflict between the Viet Minh communist insurgents and French colonial forces. And then he comes home to play house with his Vietnamese mistr</span>
  <span id="freeText1495169261512482199" style="display:none">I was pleasantly surprised how moving this story was and how strongly I warmed up to the humanity of the main character in the face of his generally detached outlook. Thomas Fowler is in a slump. As a British war correspondent working out of Saigon in French-occupied Vietnam, he gets a daily dose of duplicity and brutality in the world of ongoing guerilla conflict between the Viet Minh communist insurgents and French colonial forces. And then he comes home to play house with his Vietnamese mistress, Phuong, who is sweet and kindly prepares his opium for a nightly mental escape. Soon we come to believe that his love for Phuong is more than just selfish need. Both he and her mother would like there to be a marriage, but his estranged, Catholic wife back in London has refused to grant him a divorce. He dreads the inevitable day when he will be recalled to London for a higher position as a news editor.<br /><br />Into this scenario comes a young American, Alden Pyle, who ostensibly works for the economic development division of the U.S. legation, but in reality is a CIA spook. He is genuinely friendly to Fowler, showing a homespun Yankee hospitality in is character. From social interactions, he has become attracted to Phuong. His morality of fairness and honesty leads him to confide his interest in wooing her. He is so naÃ¯ve, the reader can only assume he is a virgin. Fowler canâ€™t help liking him. He is torn between the threat of losing her and the reluctant recognition that Phuong would probably be better off with Pyle. <br /><br />The narrative starts with this unstable situation and then alternates between Fowler reflecting back in time along various threads leading to the present and his perspective on events unfolding in the current time. As we would expect, the CIA is meddling. An academic political analyst has written a book with an idea that guides Pyle, namely that a solution for American interests requires nurturing and arming a third force among the Vietnamese as an alternative to the communists and pro-colonial sectors. This turns out to be a bumbling and dangerous strategy. The sense of decadent cynicism I saw in Fowler at the beginning is slowly replaced with great admiration for his human compassion. His actions in meeting his challenges shows a quiet bravery and a form of wisdom I think most can admire.<br /><br />As Zadie Smith points out in her preface to the book edition I accessed after doing the audiobook version, the relationship between the three characters makes for a rich personal tale of conflicts in love and loyalty while at the same time playing out the relationship between the three countries in symbolic form. Both planes hold some element of doom arising from all the complication brought on by the divergent goals of their nations and cultural differences in personal vision and morality. We know that soon the Battle of Dien Bien Phu will lead to victory by the Viet Minh and shortly thereafter withdrawal by the French. And then a few more years later, manipulations by the CIA to develop a non-communist puppet regime will lead to a much bigger war. In the precarious phase of this tale, I agree on the brilliance Zadie Smith credits Greene with in painting such a moving story in simple grays, â€œthe honest venality of Phuong, the disengagement of Fowler, the innocence of Pyle.â€<br /><br />Ultimately, what pleased me most was the bookâ€™s somehat paradoxical messages that seem prescient and modern for something written in 1955, obviously informed by Greeneâ€™s own experience as a correspondent there. Again Smith nails it that on the one hand, the blind idealism such as that which drove Pyle can lead to the evil of lives lost among the innocent, while on the other hand the fundamentally cynical view such as Fowlerâ€™s belief that ideas are not worth killing for can lead a person like him to intervene with committed actions.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1495169261512482199" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1435333086" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1435333086" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1435333086?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=borrowed-from-library">borrowed-from-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=vietnam">vietnam</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1435333086" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10950704357490779539">The Vietnam War is an era that is all too real for me. If you lived through it, you will probably agree that, as a people, we never understood what we were doing, why we were there, or who we were â€œsavingâ€. The French had already tried to remake Vietnam into a Western style democracy, and had failed entirely. This book takes place just at the passing of the baton--France has not quite given up, and America is beginning to think they have the solution.<br/><br/>That is the scene, but this book, as with all</span>
  <span id="freeText10950704357490779539" style="display:none">The Vietnam War is an era that is all too real for me. If you lived through it, you will probably agree that, as a people, we never understood what we were doing, why we were there, or who we were â€œsavingâ€. The French had already tried to remake Vietnam into a Western style democracy, and had failed entirely. This book takes place just at the passing of the baton--France has not quite given up, and America is beginning to think they have the solution.<br /><br />That is the scene, but this book, as with all of Greeneâ€™s writings, is about more than its setting, it is about people. Fowler, Pyles and Phuong are representations of the three elements that are trying to mix in Vietnam, and they are as unable to do it as individuals as they were as nations. Neither of these men understands Phuong. I was struck that she was not a real or whole person to either of them and their â€œloveâ€ for her was as selfish as love could ever be. She, on the other hand, appears to accept them as they are, without trying overly much to understand them. I think she would tell you that they are too foreign to understand--and there is the rub, they are the foreigners, she is at home.<br /><br />During one of their discussions, Fowler tells Pyle of the Vietnamese citizens: <i>"They want enough rice,' I said, 'They don't want to be shot at. They want one day to be much the same as another. They don't want our white skins around telling them what they want."</i> Fowler has been at this game long enough to understand that what the outsiders want for Vietnam is not necessarily a reflection of what the Vietnamese themselves wish for. But, while he waivers in his view from moment to moment, even he seems to see the Vietnamese as too simple and childlike to make their own choices. <br /><br />Pyle is never bothered with this struggle to see them as anything other than children, however. As the romantic imperialist, he is the guy who has all the solutions if these misguided people would just step out of his way and leave him in charge. Of course, he is deluded.<br /><br /><i> "I was to see many times that look of pain and disappointment touch his eyes and mouth when reality didn't match the romantic ideas he cherished, or when someone he loved or admired dropped below the impossible standard he had set."</i><br /><br />The essential question raised by Greene might be how much do we count? As individuals? Do some count more than others? Should one decide the fate of many? Can you witness destruction and not become involved? <br /><br />Near the end of the book, Fowler asks, "<i>How many dead colonels justify a childâ€™s or a trishaw driverâ€™s death when you are building a national democratic front?</i>"<br /><br />Sadly, I donâ€™t think we have answered that question yet.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10950704357490779539" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
