<div id="review_86653297" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/86653297" />
    <a title="Jen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1719442-jen"><img alt="Jen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451874923p2/1719442.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/86653297?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen" href="/user/show/1719442-jen">Jen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">the usual suspects</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jen by: </span>
      um. Gary? Ben?
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=lit-fic">lit-fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1719442-jen?shelf=spirituality-religion">spirituality-religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer86653297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6411811005998626726"> My time on Earth will be brief, very brief, inconsequential really to things like North America's seasonal movements, Earth's orbit, and the galaxy's star patterns. Yet I, and pretty much everyone else with as brief a life as mine, continue the search for meaning and meaningful experience (stupid humans). Are we looking for profundity in the brevity, a way to either surpass our life's span or are we simply trying to forget about its paltry duration? Birthing, dying, birthing, dying....ad infini</span>
  <span id="freeText6411811005998626726" style="display:none"> My time on Earth will be brief, very brief, inconsequential really to things like North America's seasonal movements, Earth's orbit, and the galaxy's star patterns. Yet I, and pretty much everyone else with as brief a life as mine, continue the search for meaning and meaningful experience (stupid humans). Are we looking for profundity in the brevity, a way to either surpass our life's span or are we simply trying to forget about its paltry duration? Birthing, dying, birthing, dying....ad infinitum ad nauseam. But there it is. And perhaps, more than anything else, this is true: there is an end and a beginning. A beginning and an end. To everything. Change wins. It's not always the best feeling, or a pithy sentiment one should use while patting the back of the bereaved at a funeral. <br /> Graham Greene wrote, "From childhood I had never believed in permanence, and yet I had longed for it. Always I was afraid of losing happiness. This month, next year...If not next year, in three years. Death was the only absolute value in my world. Lose life and one would lose nothing again for ever. I envied those who could believe in a God and I distrusted them. I felt they were keeping their courage up with a fable of the changeless and the permanent. Death was far more certain than God, and with death there would be no longer the daily possibility of love dying." And although not everyone can believe in God, we can all agree that the guy that wrote that so eloquently is dead. Change wins again. <br /> Despite being the kind of person who simply cannot divorce myself from the concept of God, I understand the previous paragraph. I can see how this changeless and permanent idea is an aberration, but its calmative powers are not that insignificant. The idea that there is something greater than life and death, past all effort of comprehension. To think that sense just might be made of brutality and senseless war and that if a mind was large enough, distanced enough, unclouded by stupidity or bravery, fear or pride...that maybe it can be just enough to convince some to risk doing the right thing for some of the right reasons. Was this what Greene was getting at in TQA? I may never know. But these are the things through his writing that are getting to me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6411811005998626726" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_272226690" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/272226690" />
    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/272226690?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/3642045-sue">Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Sue by: </span>
      Rebecca, Anne Reach
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=vietnam">vietnam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=library-book">library-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer272226690" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3292333410055567004">'What's the good? he'll always be innocent, you can't blame the innocent, they are always guiltless. All you can do is control them or eliminate them. Innocence is a kind of insanity.' (p155) The crux of the story the crux of the entire sad history of nations trying to remake Southeast Asia in a Western image.<br/><br/>As I read The Quiet American, I felt myself sliding down a slippery path to a very messy era I remember all too well. I was 12 in 1960 so I was a teen as the build up of the American turn i</span>
  <span id="freeText3292333410055567004" style="display:none">'What's the good? he'll always be innocent, you can't blame the innocent, they are always guiltless. All you can do is control them or eliminate them. Innocence is a kind of insanity.' (p155) The crux of the story the crux of the entire sad history of nations trying to remake Southeast Asia in a Western image.<br /><br />As I read The Quiet American, I felt myself sliding down a slippery path to a very messy era I remember all too well. I was 12 in 1960 so I was a teen as the build up of the American turn in Viet Nam occurred. Reading the comments of the French pilot, Captain Trouin, Fowler's thoughts on Pyle's "naive" but oh so intentional involvement, the story of General ThÃ©. All these brought back memories of the nightly news, Walter Cronkite, Huntley &amp; Brinkley. Of course we, the public, didn't know everything back then. It was long before the 24/7 news cycle. But the news and, particularly the photography, brought the attention of Americans to the war in a way the government would have preferred to prevent.<br /><br />Earlier in the novel, Fowler the older reporter says of Pyle:<br /><br /><br /> <i>That was my first instinct---to protect him. It never<br /> occurred to me that there was greater need to protect<br /> myself. Innocence always calls mutely for protection when<br /> we would be so much wiser to guard ourselves against it:<br /> innocence is like a dumb leper who has lost his bell,<br /> wandering the world, meaning no harm.</i> (p 29)<br /><br /><br />But there are other innocents involved too. As he thinks about his bid to get a divorce from his wife, who is back in England, Fowler reflects: <i>I had forgotten her pain for too long, and this was the only kind of recompense I could give her. Unfortunately the innocent are always involved in any conflict.</i> (p 110) <br /><br />Are these two innocents to be equated? Does Greene intend that? I can't do that though the English language provides the same word for these two people in very different situations.<br /><br />This was my first reading of The Quiet American and I found myself reliving some of those days around the TV in the mid 60s as we watched the news reports coming in from Viet Nam. Greene certainly did know that world and all the players. And he wrote this before major American involvement. It felt like I was hearing some of the same 1960s arguments on the page, indeed some of the same ones used today for exporting "democracy" around the world, whether they want it or not for, after all, "we" know what is best. Greene was a prescient writer who was able to see the future in the present and the past.<br /><br />By the last page, I felt I was in an ethical morass. There were/are no winners here. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3292333410055567004" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8189384609716796018" style="display:none"><b>4.5 âš–ï¸ âš–ï¸ âš–ï¸ âš–ï¸ âš–ï¸</b><br />For certain when you turn the pages of a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2533.Graham_Greene" title="Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">Graham Greene</a> novel itâ€™s like peeling an onion and once cut into, stimulation of your reader ducts is triggered.<br />This is a murder mystery with heavy substance as Greensâ€™s themes and motifs of innocence, detachment, morality, and religion play out through the pages which keep you pondering long after finishing.<br /><br />Protagonist Fowler thinks he has no bias, believes he has taken no side in the issues confronting him as a reporter in early 1950s Vietnam. <span id="freeTextContainer13125230517227070977"><b>On Recognising the Pattern of the Mosaic</b><br/><br/>My recollection of first reading <i>â€œThe Quiet Americanâ€</i> at school 44 years ago is that it was a work of consummate realism with a moral dimension that revolves around war and what we would now call (state-sponsored) terrorism (it was published in 1955).<br/><br/>What I hadnâ€™t recalled was how Graham Greene so skillfully structured his narrative.   <span id="freeText13125230517227070977" style="display:none"><b>On Recognising the Pattern of the Mosaic</b><br><br>My recollection of first reading <i>â€œThe Quiet Americanâ€</i> at school 44 years ago is that it was a work of consummate realism with a moral dimension that revolves around war and what we would now call (state-sponsored) terrorism (it was published in 1955).<br><br>What I hadnâ€™t recalled was how Graham Greene so skillfully structured his narrative. The chronology is fragmented, starting more or less at the end, with the assassination of the quiet American, Alden Pyle, a dispenser of foreign aid at the U.S. Legation in Saigon in the last days of French colonial rule (1954). By the time the plot returns full circle (or cycle, given the prominence of bicycles) to this event at the end of the novel, we have learned more about how it occurred and who was responsible, at least morally. <br><br>Graham Greene re-assembled the fragments into a mosaic-like structure. Thereâ€™s a sense of recognition as the shape of the mosaic becomes apparent. Although Greene wouldnâ€™t have appreciated the analogy (there was no love lost between the two men), the novel reminded me of Lawrence Durrellâ€™s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/158078357" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>â€œAlexandria Quartetâ€</i>
</a> (the first volume of which was published two years after this novel).<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1490857860i/22351533._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>Do Thi Hai Yen (as Phuong in the 2002 film)</i><br><br><b>On Running Down the American</b><br><br>The narrator, English reporter and libertine, Thomas Fowler, paints a picture of his rivalry with Pyle as one of his own age and Pyleâ€™s youth, his own experience and Pyleâ€™s innocence, his own wisdom and Pyleâ€™s naivete, and his own cynicism and Pyleâ€™s bright-eyed idealism. They happen to be rivals for the affection of a young Vietnamese woman, Phuong (with whom Fowler is living when the two men meet). Pyle wins, at least initially. Itâ€™s not clear to what extent sexual rivalry and jealousy motivate any of Fowlerâ€™s subsequent actions (Inevitably, Phuong returns to Fowler after Pyle's death). After all, Fowler acknowledges that:<br><br><i>â€œI began - almost unconsciously - to run down everything that was American...You know, it's lucky I'm not engagÃ©, there are things I might be tempted to do - because here in the East, well, I don't like Ike.â€</i><br><br><b>On Getting Involved</b><br><br>Fowler maintains a stance of objectivity, detachment and non-involvement in local politics as a condition of his journalistic ethics. He strives to be dÃ©gagÃ© rather than engagÃ©. Yet, he is warned <i>â€œabout all of us getting involved sooner or later in a moment of emotion...Sooner or later one has to take sides. If one is to remain human."</i><br><br>Fowler eventually crosses the line when he learns that Pyleâ€™s worldly innocence has lured him into a dangerous level of engagement and interference in local politics. Despite the innocuous work he seems to be doing (distributing sewing machines to the Vietnamese), it turns out that Pyle works for O.S.S. (the precursor to the C.I.A.). He has read and become enthused by the political philosophy of a fictitious American journalist/writer, York Harding (Fowler says <i>â€œHe's a superior sort of journalist - they call them diplomatic correspondents. He gets hold of an idea and then alters every situation to fit the idea...â€),</i> who highlights the role of grand abstractions like God and Democracy in global politics. They are the satin gloves that disguise the iron fist of American aggression, even before the commencement of the Vietnamese War. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1490857860i/22351534._SX540_.png" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>U.S. Embassy in Saigon (as at 1955)</i><br><br><b>On Arming the Shoddy Little Bandit</b><br><br>Pyle ends up supplying pipe and bicycle bombs to General ThÃ©, the leader of a small rebel group which he thinks will become a Third Way or Third Force in Vietnamese politics that opposes both French colonialism and Chinese-inspired communism. In contrast, Fowler describes ThÃ© as <i>â€œa shoddy little bandit with two thousand men and a couple of tame tigers.â€</i> This type of organisation couldnâ€™t survive without external financial support and weapon supplies, which O.S.S. is happy to provide from its secret budget.<br><br>Pyle isnâ€™t the worst of the Americans. That honour belongs to Bill Granger, a loud-mouthed journalist, and to Joe, the Economic Attache. The Ugly American was somebody different, a term popularised by the 1958 novel of the same name. <br><br>Certainly, Fowler retrospectively comes to regret his treatment of Pyle, even if there is nobody to whom he can say he is sorry. Not being a Catholic, he canâ€™t even go to confession. In the end, Fowler must live with his guilt...and with Phuong. Even this happy ending comes at a cost.<br><br><br><b>William Tanner Vollmann in Saigon (as Foretold by Graham Greene)(A Post-Modern Appropriation)</b><br><br><i>"â€˜You underrate yourself, Bill,â€™ the Economic Attache said. â€˜Why, that account of Road 66 - what did you call it? Highway to Hell - that was worthy of a Pulitzer. You know the story I mean - the man with his head blown off kneeling in the ditch, and that other you saw walking in a dreamâ€¦â€™ <br><br>"â€˜Do you think Iâ€™d really go near their stinking highway? Stephen Crane could describe a war without seeing one. Why shouldnâ€™t I? Itâ€™s only a damned colonial war anyway. Get me another drink. And then letâ€™s go and find a girl. Youâ€™ve got a piece of tail. I want a piece of tail too.â€™" (35)</i><br><br>March 29, 2017</span>

<div id="review_1265170137" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1265170137" />
    <a title="Max" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8079403-max"><img alt="Max" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1344194443p2/8079403.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1265170137?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Max" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Max" href="/user/show/8079403-max">Max</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8079403-max?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1265170137" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13887734579332128562">Greene intertwines two mirror image triangular relationships in early 1950â€™s Viet Nam. The fictional plot is centered on a real event, a car bombing in a busy downtown Saigon square in January 1952. The French SÃ»retÃ© blamed the rogue nationalist Colonel Tháº¿. The American mission blamed the Viet Minh. Greene was a war correspondent based in Saigon at the time and many scenes in the book are drawn from things he witnessed. He started writing the book in March 1952. <br/><br/>Greeneâ€™s first triangle is perso</span>
  <span id="freeText13887734579332128562" style="display:none">Greene intertwines two mirror image triangular relationships in early 1950â€™s Viet Nam. The fictional plot is centered on a real event, a car bombing in a busy downtown Saigon square in January 1952. The French SÃ»retÃ© blamed the rogue nationalist Colonel Tháº¿. The American mission blamed the Viet Minh. Greene was a war correspondent based in Saigon at the time and many scenes in the book are drawn from things he witnessed. He started writing the book in March 1952. <br /><br />Greeneâ€™s first triangle is personal. The young impetuous American Alden Pyle falls in love with Phuong, the beautiful Vietnamese mistress of English reporter Thomas Fowler. The second triangle is national. Headstrong CIA Agent, Alden Pyle, leads an American attempt to establish a third force led by Colonel Tháº¿ against both the French and the Viet Minh. The same values and motives underlie both situations. <br /><br />The first side of the triangles is the self-indulgent Thomas Fowler. Fowler is in love with Phuong but keeps her as his mistress since he is married although separated from his wife in England. He has a cynical view of the war, the desires of the Vietnamese people and of Phuong, who he uses just as Viet Nam is used by its European master. Fowler sees no morality on any side, the French, the Viet Minh or the American. His motto is just let it be, interference just adds to the death and injured toll and accomplishes nothing. His views are those of the colonial rulers who see their empires collapsing and take refuge in their smugness and legacy of privilege.<br /><br />The second side of the triangles is the impulsive Alden Pyle, who offers to marry Phuong and take her away from the decadent Fowler and chaotic Viet Nam to his home in Boston where he believes she will thrive as a middle class housewife. Also for the purest of motives he hopes to launch a third force in the war to do away with both the evil communists and the colonial puppet government and bring democracy to the Vietnamese. Pyle is portrayed as too simple to see Phuongâ€™s true nature - that she would not fit in conventional New England. Similarly he is shown as too simple to understand the complexities of the Vietnamese people and the war - that American style democracy would not work in their culture. <br /><br />The third side of the triangles is Phuong, but we only get Fowlerâ€™s jaded picture. Just as Greene portrays his idea of a prototypical American (albeit very fitting as events turned out) so he gives us his conception of a prototypical Asian woman who never reveals her true self - mysterious and alluring, always accommodating, but underneath clever and pragmatic. Fowler needs Phuong to light his opium pipes and take care of him as he grows old, just as England and France need the colonies to support them as they decline. He sees Phuong as someone interested solely in security. Young and handsome or old and paunchy does not matter, only who will provide for her and be reliable. <br /> <br />Greeneâ€™s book tells us as much about Greene, as it does about Viet Nam, its people and the war. He was in MI6 during WWII, reporting to his friend secret Soviet agent Kim Philby. Some French officials suspected Greene was employed by MI6 while in Viet Nam, which he denied. He reported on the war for <i>The Times</i> and <i>Le Figaro</i> from 1951 -1954. Greene loved his time in Viet Nam and clearly patterned Fowler after himself. Greene particularly liked the restaurants, the nightlife, the opium, and the prostitutes. Oxford educated, aloof and self-absorbed, he reflects the British Empireâ€™s administrative class alarm at upstart Americaâ€™s interference in world affairs previously dominated by European powers. Fowlerâ€™s view of Phuong and by extension the Vietnamese represents Greeneâ€™s colonialist image of the Asian. Greene was right about Americaâ€™s simplicity in getting involved in the Indochina war. However, his characterization of Phuong and the oriental is also simplistic - and racist as well. In spite of Greeneâ€™s presumptiveness and classist views, The Quiet American is well worth reading. Greene is a skillful writer giving us fine prose, a cleverly constructed plot and a vivid sense of an exotic time and place he knew well. <br /><br />An interesting aside is the plot change in the 1958 Joseph Mankiewicz movie made to assuage politically powerful pro Diem activist groups in the US. Diem had strong support from the likes of Henry Luce and Cardinal Spellman. In the novel Greene had the American (Alden Pyle) arrange the bombing to help create a third force. American diplomats Greene knew were discussing a third force strategy at the time the novel was written. Mankiewicz instead made the English reporter, Fowler, a communist dupe who helps the communists plan the bombing. Needless to say Greene was very upset by the switch which completely changed the novelâ€™s message calling it â€œa propaganda film for Americaâ€. The 2002 remake is more faithful to the novelâ€™s plot. While Greene later recanted his support for the French, he never changed his opinion of America or Americaâ€™s responsibility for the 1952 bombing.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13887734579332128562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3008188403" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8051792-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544530609p2/8051792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3008188403?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/8051792-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3008188403" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8801858504080659146">[4+] <i>The Quiet American</i> could be called "The Ignorant American" or "The Arrogant American" but the wry title sets the tone of the novel. Written in 1955, the novel is a searing look at the beginning of U.S. involvement in Vietnam through the eyes of a war-weary English journalist. If only we had heeded Greene's cautionary message! I'm glad my book club is going to discuss this novel because there is so much going on under the surface to talk about.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1396689847" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1396689847" />
    <a title="[P]" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40516522-p"><img alt="[P]" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1486240560p2/40516522.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1396689847?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="[P]" class="user" itemprop="url" name="[P]" href="/user/show/40516522-p">[P]</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/29639.The_Quiet_American">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1396689847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer623587890948040487">Earlier this year I was in Prague visiting a friend of mine. My personal circumstances havenâ€™t been the best for the last twelve months and I had slipped into a state of deep depression without realising it. The purpose of this trip was to get away from everything, to drink a lot and lose myself in that beautiful city. One afternoon my friend and I were in a bar, six drinks deep and thrillingly relaxed. That is, until a group of Americans arrived. They took the table behind us, and began to figh</span>
  <span id="freeText623587890948040487" style="display:none">Earlier this year I was in Prague visiting a friend of mine. My personal circumstances havenâ€™t been the best for the last twelve months and I had slipped into a state of deep depression without realising it. The purpose of this trip was to get away from everything, to drink a lot and lose myself in that beautiful city. One afternoon my friend and I were in a bar, six drinks deep and thrillingly relaxed. That is, until a group of Americans arrived. They took the table behind us, and began to fight for each otherâ€™s attention like a bunch of rambunctious puppies. â€˜I hate them,â€™ my friend said quietly, and at first I thought he meant only this particular group, until he followed up with â€˜fucking Americans, I canâ€™t stand them.â€™ It wasnâ€™t the first time I had heard someone dismiss an entire nation, but I was still surprised by this passionate outburst. Of course, I was aware of the stereotype of the brash and grossly impolite and uncultured American, but I had never really given it much thought, and, with â€˜yanksâ€™ being in short supply in Sheffield, I certainly hadnâ€™t before heard such vitriol directed at them. â€˜Theyâ€™ve probably come over here to start a war,â€™ my friend seethed.<br><br>Since returning from Prague, and now particularly sensitive to it, I have come to realise that this negative stereotype is fairly common amongst the English, and this was at least partly the reason why I have been so interested in reading Graham Greeneâ€™s 1955 novel, The Quiet American. It is worth noting in this regard that the title itself could be interpreted as a sly form of mockery, in that it speaks with an element of surprise, as though a quiet American is a rare thing. The American in question is Alden Pyle, a young man with an â€˜unused faceâ€™, who arrives in war-stricken Vietnam, seemingly as some kind of charity or aid worker, and quickly befriends an Englishman, Thomas Fowler, and his native girlfriend, Phuong. This triangle comes to dominate the novel, and has both political and personal repercussions.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1442760807i/16275932._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The Quiet American is narrated by Fowler, and he describes Pyle numerous times as naÃ¯ve and innocent. Moreover, the young man himself admits that he lacks experience, especially with women. In his early interactions with Phuong he is excessively polite. He pulls out her chair for her in a bar and, as they sit around a table, he objects to what he considers to be indiscreet conversation, the kind not suitable for a womanâ€™s ears. It is clear that for Pyle women, or Vietnamese women at least, ought to be protected, that he sees them as delicate creatures or even almost as children. Indeed, he is disproportionately affected when one of his fellow countrymen visits a brothel. This man, Granger, is the archetypal loud American, a straight-talking, bullish and arrogant Philadelphian, with whom Fowler occasionally locks horns.<br><br>While it seems as though Pyle is a sweet, harmless, candid, heart-on-the-sleeve kind of guy, with his whole life ahead of him, Fowler is an ageing journalist with a developing paunch and a wife back in England. In contrast to his starry-eyed young friend, Fowlerâ€™s predominant attitude is a kind of disgruntled, world-weariness. Indeed, he claims to only want 18 year old Phuong in order to fight off the loneliness of old-age. To this end, the arrival of Pyle is the worst thing that could have happened to him, because his new friend falls in love with her and becomes intent on marrying her. Predictably, Pyleâ€™s love for Phuong is idealistic, as is his approach to his rival. He claims that he wants to do the right and honourable thing, for example, he undergoes extreme danger in order to go to Fowler and reveal to him his feelings for the manâ€™s girlfriend. Significantly, both in terms of understanding Pyle and the novel as a whole, Fowler asks him why he doesnâ€™t just leave without telling Phuong about his love, why he doesnâ€™t want to avoid causing trouble, and Pyle responds by saying that this wouldnâ€™t be fair.<br><br><blockquote>â€œI wish sometimes you had a few bad motives, you might understand a little more about human beings.â€</blockquote><br><br>For Alden Pyle the consequences of his actions are less important than his intention. His intention is to do the right thing, and so if people get hurt that is simply an unfortunate, regrettable, but unavoidable form of collateral damage. What is paramount is that he acted in accordance with his principles. Fowler, on the other hand, understands that things are never that clear cut, that a good man trying to do good can, as a result, do bad things, can cause harm, which in this instance would be to hurt Fowler and possibly Phuong also. I found all this fascinating. One never doubts that Pyle is in earnest, that he is on the level, that he is a nice guy, he is simply â€œimpregnably armored by his good intentions and his ignorance.â€ His character flaw is refusing to accept, or to see, the world as it is.<br><br>I wrote earlier about personal and political repercussions, and it is interesting, and satisfying, how Greene uses this love triangle to mirror the political situation in the country. Both Pyle and Fowler are outsiders, or invaders if you like, fighting over a Vietnamese, and while the American may be frequently described as innocent, the only real innocent in the situation is Phuong, who comes to represent the ordinary civilian during the war. Moreover, it is not surprising that Pyle brings the same attitude towards his job, which, we come to realise, is not as an aid worker, but a kind of terrorist working for the American government. Again, Pyleâ€™s dangerous idealism, his naivety, means that he harms while trying to do good or he justifies harm in the name of what is good. The line between terrorist and liberator is, for him, not a thin one, it is clear and pronounced. Greeneâ€™s point appears to be that this is the American mind-set, that America wades into conflicts with the best intentions in the world, without comprehending the extent of the damage they are causing or likely to cause.<br><br><blockquote>â€œInnocence always calls mutely for protection when we would be so much wiser to guard ourselves against it: innocence is like a dumb leper who has lost his bell, wandering the world, meaning no harm.â€</blockquote><br><br>However, while I can see why he thought this, and I agree to an extent, I, ironically, think he was being too naive himself [unless of course I have misunderstood him]. In terms of individual soldiers, then, yes, Iâ€™ve met quite a few and they have all been absolutely convinced that what they are doing â€“ in Iraq, Afghanistan etc â€“ is entirely positive, that they are helping these poor downtrodden countries, that they are bringing democracy to them, and that this is a wonderful thing, even if they have to kill thousands of innocent people in order to do it. What I donâ€™t accept is that the real people in power in America, the people who sanction these conflicts, who send these individuals into these countries, are like Pyle, I donâ€™t buy that they are the Goofy, â€˜aw shucksâ€™ variety. I believe that the people who sanction war know exactly what they are doing and why they are doing it. Power, greed, money, these are the things that drive foreign policy. Oh sure, weâ€™ll get told that, for example, communism is a threat to world security, but the real threat it poses is to certain peopleâ€™s bank balances; likewise, human rights violations are never the reason we engage. The American [and British] government donâ€™t give a single, shiny fuck about human rights violations.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1442760558i/16275917.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>One further potential flaw with The Quiet American is that the friendship between the two men comes across as forced, certainly on Pyleâ€™s side. He speaks about Fowler being his best friend, even though they have known each other for only a very short time. He compliments the man frequently and claims to understand him, to such an extent that it just does not ring true. However, this isnâ€™t necessarily a failure of Greeneâ€™s, it could be justified in line with the bookâ€™s themes. Isnâ€™t Pyleâ€™s insistence that Fowler is a good man, that the men have bonded and are great friends, a sign of his immaturity? One could even argue that it is the arrogance of the American, one that believes that he can make friends so easily and can understand other people better than they understand themselves. In terms of Fowler, his affection makes sense. He appreciates Pyleâ€™s wide-eyed approach to life, which is so different from his own; but he never considers them to be bosom buddies like Pyle does.<br><br>Iâ€™ve written a lot about Pyle in this review, and I do think that he is a wonderful creation, but, for me, it is through Fowler that Greene raises the most engaging and important question. As previously noted, he is in Vietnam to report on the war between the French and the Viet Mihn communist-nationalist revolutionaries. Fowler, according to himself, steadfastly refuses to take sides, going so far as to say that he has no opinions on what is happening in the country. As The Quiet American pushes on towards its moving conclusion, Greene asks â€˜is it possible to not become involved? Can you watch people being killed and not have an opinion?â€™ This is something that I ask of people all the time, most recently with the refugee crisis. Can you remain neutral in the face of overwhelming suffering? I know I canâ€™t. And neither, ultimately, can Fowler, who is forced to throw off his moral cowardice and act. I wonâ€™t reveal what he does, or the consequences of what he does, but it is worth noting that the decision to act is justified in almost exactly the same way that Pyle justifies his own actions, in that it involves the sacrifice of life for, the argument states, the greater good. Perhaps then the only thing one can say with any certainty where war is concerned is that there are no absolutes, no easy answers, it is, and will remain, a messy, horrible, horrifying state of affairs. Much like love, I guess.</span>

<div id="review_875239493" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/875239493" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20595982-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438164755p2/20595982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/875239493?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/20595982-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20595982-alex?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20595982-alex?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer875239493" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10861787271481745068">On the weekend, I came across a box of books belonging to my late brother. It's well over three years since his passing and I thought I had "unpacked" his belongings that still hold hostage to my garage. This box contained many gems, on the top was sitting <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3698.The_Quiet_American' title='The Quiet American by Graham Greene' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Quiet American</a>. As one does, I started reading the first paragraph. By the next day I had finished it, astonished that I had not gotten to reading the work of my brother's favourite author.<br/><br/>In death, my brother has moved into a sort of hero</span>
  <span id="freeText10861787271481745068" style="display:none">On the weekend, I came across a box of books belonging to my late brother. It's well over three years since his passing and I thought I had "unpacked" his belongings that still hold hostage to my garage. This box contained many gems, on the top was sitting <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3698.The_Quiet_American" title="The Quiet American by Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">The Quiet American</a>. As one does, I started reading the first paragraph. By the next day I had finished it, astonished that I had not gotten to reading the work of my brother's favourite author.<br /><br />In death, my brother has moved into a sort of heroic realm for me: he lived and worked as an academic in South East Asia, from a base in Kuala Lumpur. He forged a great and interesting life, not afraid to take risks in search of finding his place. We had had many literary discussions over the year, a shared love of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/585.John_Steinbeck" title="John Steinbeck" rel="nofollow noopener">John Steinbeck</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/22595.Peter_Carey" title="Peter Carey" rel="nofollow noopener">Peter Carey</a> always at the fore. <br /><br />This is such a brilliant tale of love, jealousy, politics, war and innocence that it is hard to think such weighty themes can be given such scrutiny within a tiny 180 pages. Greene published this book set in Vietnam during the build up to what became the Vietnam war in 1955 which in itself is eery in that the ideas expressed about America's role in South East Asia was proven to be true as the 60s and 70s came.<br /><br />Thomas Fowler is a cynical, world weary British journalist stationed in Vietnam, reporting the basics of what was occurring based on the "favourable" press conferences held by the French Imperialist forces. In the sweat and grime of Saigon, he has escaped a loveless marriage in the UK and fallen in love (if he is capable of such an emotion) with a 20 year old Vietnamese dancer, Phuong. Into his life comes the seemingly benignly idealistic "Quiet American", Alden Pyle. Much younger than Fowler, both his youthful self confidence and his slavish adherence to a new US socio-political policy involving the eradication of Communism threatens the life Fowler has made for himself. Something has to give.<br /><br />Greene expertly crafts his story by alternating chapters: one being the present and future, the next the present looking into the past. The story moves along at a cracking pace, with opportunities for the characters to interact in such a way that macro political ideas are expressed in a personal way. This is particularly shown in the conversations between Fowler and the French policeman, Fowler and the French pilot as well as between Fowler and Pyle.<br /><br />Brilliant and moving, I can see why my brother was a devotee of Greene's.<br /><br />PS. Under his copy of this book are copies of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48862.Brighton_Rock" title="Brighton Rock by Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">Brighton Rock</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29641.The_End_of_the_Affair" title="The End of the Affair by Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">The End of the Affair</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3690.The_Power_and_the_Glory" title="The Power and the Glory by Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">The Power and the Glory</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/133394.Our_Man_in_Havana" title="Our Man in Havana by Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">Our Man in Havana</a>. These now demand to be read - I'm getting to know my brother again :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="10861787271481745068" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Gabrielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle"><img alt="Gabrielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598976963p2/6753400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1856777953?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabrielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabrielle" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle">Gabrielle</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1856777953" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6414333617953303205">4 and a half stars, rounded up. This is a novel about the good intentions that pave the road to Hellâ€¦<br/><br/>Greeneâ€™s prose is truly beautiful, simple yet elegant, and I couldnâ€™t stop thinking about how eerie it was that he penned this novel in 1955, making it more than a bit prophetic: it could be looked at either as a strange love triangle, or as a metaphor for the way naÃ¯ve, well-meaning Americans clumsily try to help out people they perceive as less privileged or sophisticated than them â€“ and wreck </span>
  <span id="freeText6414333617953303205" style="display:none">4 and a half stars, rounded up. This is a novel about the good intentions that pave the road to Hellâ€¦<br /><br />Greeneâ€™s prose is truly beautiful, simple yet elegant, and I couldnâ€™t stop thinking about how eerie it was that he penned this novel in 1955, making it more than a bit prophetic: it could be looked at either as a strange love triangle, or as a metaphor for the way naÃ¯ve, well-meaning Americans clumsily try to help out people they perceive as less privileged or sophisticated than them â€“ and wreck everything in the process.<br /><br />Post-WWII Britain was on the decline: exhausted, struggling (and failing) to keep its various colonies in check, not sure how to really find its place in the 20th century; as where post-WWII â€˜Murica was triumphantly buoyed by its newly acquired image as liberators of the oppressed. They had just saved the day in Europe, right? They could go around and do that everywhere now! Woohoo!<br /><br />Alden Pyle is an enthusiastic, ambitious but misguided young CIA operative, whoâ€™s learned everything he knows about Viet-Nam and whatâ€™s going on there in books. Thomas Fowler is a cynical, world-weary, opium-smoking British journalist who has worked as a war correspondent based in Viet-Nam for years. He befriends the eager and idealistic Pyle â€“ an odd friendship if there ever was one, as they both view the country they find themselves in very differently. This is illustrated by the way they see and treat Phuong, Fowlerâ€™s much younger Vietnamese mistress. Phuong is in many ways like a pet to Fowler: he is very fond of her, wants to take care of her and protect her, but he mostly keeps her around because heâ€™s lonely and probably fears dying alone. To Pyle, sheâ€™s an exotic beauty in need of rescue, and in his mind, nothing could make her happier than becoming the wife of an American and relocating to the Land of the Free.<br /><br />I found myself wondering: who the Hell is this woman, anyway? Cuz neither one of these guys see her for what she really is. And since this story is told from Fowlerâ€™s point of view, we never get to know either! Is she really being used by Fowler, or does she know exactly what sheâ€™s doing? Does she really prefer Pyle to him, or is she just doing whatâ€™s in her best self-interest? Is she playing those two men, who both objectify and underestimate her in different ways, against each other?<br /><br />Her lack of development was the only thing that irked me with this book: aside from that, it's a nearly perfect novel, beautifully constructed and cleverly paced. I read â€œThe End of the Affairâ€ years ago, and to be honest, I forgot almost everything about it besides the broad strokes of the story and a distinct feeling that while I had enjoyed the prose, the story irritated me insufferably (and yet I gave it 4 stars? Iâ€™ll have to revisit it and clear this upâ€¦). Probably because of the Catholic thing making everyone miserable. Or because I am so over the theme of obsessive love/lust. I was very relieved to find â€œThe Quiet Americanâ€ a much richer and more sophisticated story, and while the characters can be pathetic at times, they are not as infuriating as Bendrix and Sarah were.<br /><br />A quick but wonderful read. Highly recommended!</span>

<div id="review_38911566" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38911566" />
    <a title="Jason Koivu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu"><img alt="Jason Koivu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560521450p2/1742824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/38911566?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Koivu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Koivu" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu">Jason Koivu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer38911566" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5788919631271680127">Relationships are complicated by human failings. It's one of British author Graham Greene's themes, and it's fair enough and true. And in Green's world a happy ending is, at best, an ambivalent one. This would explain why I have such a hard time enjoying his books.<br/><br/>He was a great writer. His stories often get to the heart of the matter, eventually. The problem is, he wrote so accurately about human behavior as to make his novels quite trying to one's patience. If you're looking for flawed charact</span>
  <span id="freeText5788919631271680127" style="display:none">Relationships are complicated by human failings. It's one of British author Graham Greene's themes, and it's fair enough and true. And in Green's world a happy ending is, at best, an ambivalent one. This would explain why I have such a hard time enjoying his books.<br /><br />He was a great writer. His stories often get to the heart of the matter, eventually. The problem is, he wrote so accurately about human behavior as to make his novels quite trying to one's patience. If you're looking for flawed characters making bad choices for psychologically sound reasons, at least in their own minds, well then you've come to the right shop. <br /><br /><i>The Quiet American</i> is set during France's "Vietnam War", the one before America's. I believe it was called the First Indochina War, and it stretched from the mid '40s to the mid '50s. This book reads like a news article forecasting a coming war, for it focuses on an American militant outlier's involvement in a conflict well before the U.S. government would eventually get involved. <br /><br />The story follows a British journalist covering the war, who meets a seemingly naive and mysterious American with idealized notions of what's best of the native population, and who swoops in and steals the Brit's bit of foreign good-time fluff. The American's off-the-cuff charm, the Brit's loveless love, and the aloofness of Vietnamese love interest that finishes of the love triangle, all three of these principle participants are mostly in it for themselves, for their own motives, but they are neither good nor bad people. They are just people. <br /><br />The military conflict mirrors the human relationship, and the same questions can be asked of both situations: "What are you doing here?" and "Why are you interfering?"<br /><br />I keep trying to enjoy Greene's books, but it just ain't happening. I mean, yeah I gave this 4 stars (it would be a 3.5 if I could use halves) because it is good writing. However, it's just never thoroughly enjoyable. There's always a certain "gloom" about his work. It's often slow, too, though it never grinds to a complete halt. However, I will continue reading Greene, because it deserves to be read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5788919631271680127" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_10271641" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10271641" />
    <a title="Karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/568411-karen"><img alt="Karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267907021p2/568411.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10271641?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen" href="/user/show/568411-karen">Karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10271641" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16080550081306839533">As a critique of American intervention in foreign affairs, the story was excellent. The "quiet" American (he never shuts up) steps into a world he knows nothing about and creates havoc. <br/><br/>My problem with the book was a problem common to many similar authors (DeLillo, I'm looking at you): it was very male-centric and I got annoyed. Phuong, the love/lust/possession interest in the book, was never given a character, described as innocent, childish, a sexual object, and a caretaker in turn. I realize </span>
  <span id="freeText16080550081306839533" style="display:none">As a critique of American intervention in foreign affairs, the story was excellent. The "quiet" American (he never shuts up) steps into a world he knows nothing about and creates havoc. <br /><br />My problem with the book was a problem common to many similar authors (DeLillo, I'm looking at you): it was very male-centric and I got annoyed. Phuong, the love/lust/possession interest in the book, was never given a character, described as innocent, childish, a sexual object, and a caretaker in turn. I realize the character was supposed to be symbolic of the country--both men, American and British, wanted to possess her, without understanding her. But it's hard to read a book where women aren't allowed to be women--full fleshed, intelligent and capable of self determination.<br /><br />Here, I was doing acrobatics to make the story palatable. It reminded me of reading Lolita. During both books, it was an interesting character study of the people watching the women, if you considered it from the woman's perspective. I still felt slimy reading it. I get that the sliminess is the point, but ew.<br /><br />It makes me grateful for books like "Regeneration" which make important points, but with all the characters fully drawn. (It's my new favorite book this year.)<br /><br />In any case, I'm glad I read it. The writing is beautiful and it tells an important story. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16080550081306839533" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

	<tr>
		<td>
		    <b>
          <a title="Reading the 20th Century" class="actionLink" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/21703775-the-quiet-american-by-graham-greene-december-2020">Reading the 20th ...:</a>
        </b>
        <img class="topicPadlock" border="0" alt="This topic has been closed to new comments." src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/padlock-2bbe3d7fcd0f2c2e591eeb32c3243491.png" />
      
			<a class="actionLink" href="/topic/show/21703775-the-quiet-american-by-graham-greene-december-2020">The Quiet American by Graham Greene (December 2020)</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jCVPyDp9mWnsTDzBbqQFGYC9squFhKZUqseEE5ZCvSG2AyYagSNQAUtniKCZUQ/GVuJVY0sJKDPxucKiWwFPuw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('uxV7WCywaAaBx5cPoPXe/pkaT2K5Sd2emlrr7IUNC8WBMxKKl+6hbibsI25XANQhT0WoqnfEU/nBJK1dSE75Xw==')})
  }

Waugh in Abyssinia is an entertaining account by a cantankerous and unenthusiastic war reporter that provides a fascinating short history of Mussolini\'s imperial adventure as well as a wickedly witty preview of the characters and follies that figure into Waugh\'s famous satire. In the forward, veteran foreign correspondent John Maxwell Hamilton explores in how Waugh ended up in Abyssinia, which real-life events were fictionalized in Scoop, and how this memoir fits into Waugh\'s overall literary career, which includes the classic Brideshead Revisited. As Hamilton explains, Waugh was the right man (a misfit), in the right place (a largely unknown country that lent itself to farcical imagination), at the right time (when the correspondents themselves were more interesting than the scraps of news they could get.) The result is Pole to Pole, Palin\'s account of his extraordinary journey between July and December 1991, passing through 17 countries from Greenland and the former Soviet Union in the north to Kenya, South Africa and Chile in the south. From the frozen wastes of both poles, to the scorching heat of Africa, Pole to Pole is a travelogue of bizarre extremes. Palin revels in the surrealism of it all as he travels through a range of vastly different European and African communities undergoing massive social and political upheavals in the aftermath of the fall of the Berlin Wall. Palin\'s shrewd observations are as ever interspersed with his eye for the weird and the comical, as he meets Santa Claus and Lenin, goes shopping for camels in Omdurman, and makes a final hectic dash to the South Pole via Chile. <span id="freeTextContainer16689722263895679772">Henry Graham Greene, OM, CH was an English novelist, short story writer, playwright, screenplay writer, travel writer and critic whose works explore the ambivalent moral and political issues of the modern world. Greene combined serious literary acclaim with wide popularity.<br/><br/>Although Greene objected strongly to being described as a â€œCatholic novelistâ€ rather than as a â€œnovelist who happened to be Ca</span>
  <span id="freeText16689722263895679772" style="display:none">Henry Graham Greene, OM, CH was an English novelist, short story writer, playwright, screenplay writer, travel writer and critic whose works explore the ambivalent moral and political issues of the modern world. Greene combined serious literary acclaim with wide popularity.<br /><br />Although Greene objected strongly to being described as a â€œCatholic novelistâ€ rather than as a â€œnovelist who happened to be Catholic,â€ Catholic religious themes are at the root of much of his writing, especially the four major Catholic novels: <i>Brighton Rock, The Heart of the Matter, The End of the Affair,</i> and <i>The Power and the Glory.</i> Works such as <i>The Quiet American, Our Man in Havana</i> and <i>The Human Factor</i> also show an avid interest in the workings of international politics and espionage.<br /><br />(<i>Excerpted from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graham_Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia</a>)</i></span>
